				<div class="minerva__subtitle"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">&#160;&#160;(Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express_Co&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="American Express Co">American Express Co</a>)</span></div>
			</div>
			
			<div id="bodyContent" class="content"><div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><script>function mfTempOpenSection(id){var block=document.getElementById("mf-section-"+id);block.className+=" open-block";block.previousSibling.className+=" open-block";}</script><div class="mw-parser-output"><section class="mf-section-0" id="mf-section-0"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Amex" redirects here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/AMEX_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="AMEX (disambiguation)">AMEX</a>.</div>
<p>The <b>American Express Company</b>, also known as <b>Amex</b>, is an American <a href="/wiki/Multinational_corporation" title="Multinational corporation">multinational</a> <a href="/wiki/Financial_services" title="Financial services">financial services</a> corporation headquartered at <a href="/wiki/200_Vesey_Street" title="200 Vesey Street">200 Vesey Street</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>. The company was founded in 1850 and is one of the 30 components of the <a href="/wiki/Dow_Jones_Industrial_Average" title="Dow Jones Industrial Average">Dow Jones Industrial Average</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> The company is best known for its <a href="/wiki/Charge_card" title="Charge card">charge card</a>, <a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">credit card</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Traveler%27s_cheque" title="Traveler's cheque">traveler's cheque</a> businesses.

equity</a></span></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><img alt="Increase" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/11px-Increase2.svg.png" decoding="async" title="Increase" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/17px-Increase2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/Increase2.svg/22px-Increase2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="300" data-file-height="300"> <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$22.290 billion</span> <span style="font-size:85%;">(2018)<sup id="cite_ref-10-K_1-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10-K-1">[1]</a></sup></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;"><div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">Number of employees</div></th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;">59,000 <span style="font-size:85%;">(December 2018)<sup id="cite_ref-10-K_1-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10-K-1">[1]</a></sup></span></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" style="padding-right: 0.5em;">Website</th><td style="line-height: 1.35em;"><span class="url"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.americanexpress.com/">www<wbr></wbr>.americanexpress<wbr></wbr>.com</a></span></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;line-height: 1.35em;"><b>Footnotes / references</b><br><sup id="cite_ref-PZG_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PZG-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NML_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NML-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10-K_2019_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10-K_2019-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table><p>In 2016, credit cards using the American Express network accounted for 22.9% of the total dollar volume of credit card transactions in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> As of December 31, 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the company had 114.4<span class="nowrap"> </span>million cards in force, including 54.7<span class="nowrap"> </span>million cards in force in the United States, each with an average annual spending of $19,972.<sup id="cite_ref-10-K_2019_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10-K_2019-4">[4]</a></sup></p><p>In 2017, <i><a href="/wiki/Forbes" title="Forbes">Forbes</a></i> named American Express as the 23rd most valuable brand in the world (and the highest within financial services), estimating the brand to be worth US$24.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion.<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In 2020, <i><a href="/wiki/Fortune_(magazine)" title="Fortune (magazine)">Fortune</a></i> magazine ranked American Express at number 9 on their Fortune List of the Top 100 Companies to Work For in 2020 based on an employee survey of satisfaction.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup></p><p>The company's <a href="/wiki/Logo" title="Logo">logo</a>, adopted in 1958, is a <a href="/wiki/Gladiator" title="Gladiator">gladiator</a> or <a href="/wiki/Centurion" title="Centurion">centurion</a><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> whose image appears on the company's <a href="/wiki/Traveler%27s_cheque" title="Traveler's cheque">traveler's cheques</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charge_card" title="Charge card">charge cards</a> and <a href="/wiki/Credit_card" title="Credit card">credit cards</a>.
</p>

</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(1)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history">Early history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early history" data-section="1" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-1 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-1">
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:287px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Express_Shipping_Receipt_1853.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/American_Express_Shipping_Receipt_1853.jpg/285px-American_Express_Shipping_Receipt_1853.jpg" decoding="async" width="285" height="310" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1187" data-file-height="1292"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 285px;height: 310px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/American_Express_Shipping_Receipt_1853.jpg/285px-American_Express_Shipping_Receipt_1853.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="285" data-height="310" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Express_Shipping_Receipt_1853.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>American Express Co. early shipping <a href="/wiki/Receipt" title="Receipt">receipts</a> (1853, 1869)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Express_Company_1865.JPG" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/American_Express_Company_1865.JPG/220px-American_Express_Company_1865.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="159" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="3245" data-file-height="2339"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 220px;height: 159px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/American_Express_Company_1865.JPG/220px-American_Express_Company_1865.JPG" data-alt="" data-width="220" data-height="159" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Express_Company_1865.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Share of the American Express Company, issued 13. October 1865; signed by William G. Fargo as Secretary and Henry Wells as President</div></div></div>
<p>In 1850, American Express was started as an <a href="/wiki/Express_mail" title="Express mail">express mail</a> business in <a href="/wiki/Buffalo,_New_York" title="Buffalo, New York">Buffalo, New York</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> It was founded as a joint stock corporation by the merger of the express companies owned by <a href="/wiki/Henry_Wells" title="Henry Wells">Henry Wells</a> (Wells &amp; Company), <a href="/wiki/William_G._Fargo" class="mw-redirect" title="William G. Fargo">William G. Fargo</a> (Livingston, Fargo &amp; Company), and <a href="/wiki/John_Warren_Butterfield" class="mw-redirect" title="John Warren Butterfield">John Warren Butterfield</a> (Wells, Butterfield &amp; Company, the successor earlier in 1850 of Butterfield, Wasson &amp; Company).<sup id="cite_ref-PZG_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PZG-2">[2]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NML_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NML-3">[3]</a></sup> Wells and Fargo also started <a href="/wiki/History_of_Wells_Fargo" title="History of Wells Fargo">Wells Fargo &amp; Co.</a> in 1852 when Butterfield and other directors objected to the proposal that American Express extend its operations to California.
</p><p>American Express initially established its headquarters in a building at the intersection of Jay Street and <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Hudson Street (Manhattan)">Hudson Street</a> in what was later called the <a href="/wiki/Tribeca" title="Tribeca">Tribeca</a> section of Manhattan. For years it enjoyed a virtual monopoly on the movement of express shipments (goods, securities, currency, etc.) throughout New York State. In 1874, American Express moved its headquarters to 65 Broadway in what was becoming the <a href="/wiki/Financial_District,_Manhattan" title="Financial District, Manhattan">Financial District</a> of Manhattan, a location it was to retain through two buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Citycyclopedia_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Citycyclopedia-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="American_Express_buildings">American Express buildings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: American Express buildings" data-section="2" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1854, the American Express Co. purchased a lot on Vesey Street in New York City as the site for its stables. The company's first New York headquarters was an 1858 marble Italianate palazzo at 55–61 <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Hudson Street (Manhattan)">Hudson Street</a>, which had a busy freight depot on the ground story with a spur line from the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_River_Railroad" class="mw-redirect" title="Hudson River Railroad">Hudson River Railroad</a>. A stable was constructed in 1867, five blocks north at 4–8 Hubert Street.
</p><p>The company prospered sufficiently that headquarters were moved in 1874 from the wholesale shipping district to the budding Financial District, and into rented offices in two five-story brownstone commercial buildings at 63 and 65 Broadway that were owned by the Harmony family.<sup id="cite_ref-LANDMARKS_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANDMARKS-12">[12]</a></sup></p><p>In 1880, American Express built a new warehouse behind the Broadway Building at 46 Trinity Place. The designer is unknown, but it has a façade of brick arches that are reminiscent of pre-skyscraper New York. American Express has long been out of this building, but it still bears a <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> seal with the American Express Eagle.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> In 1890–91 the company constructed a new ten-story building by <a href="/wiki/Edward_H._Kendall" title="Edward H. Kendall">Edward H. Kendall</a> on the site of its former headquarters on <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Street_(Manhattan)" title="Hudson Street (Manhattan)">Hudson Street</a>.
</p><p>By 1903, the company had assets of some $28 million, second only to the <a href="/wiki/Citibank" title="Citibank">National City Bank of New York</a> among <a href="/wiki/Financial_institution" title="Financial institution">financial institutions</a> in the city. To reflect this, the company purchased the Broadway buildings and site.<sup id="cite_ref-LANDMARKS_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANDMARKS-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Express_Company_Building_65_Broadway_entrance.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/American_Express_Company_Building_65_Broadway_entrance.jpg/250px-American_Express_Company_Building_65_Broadway_entrance.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="178" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="2062" data-file-height="1470"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 250px;height: 178px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/American_Express_Company_Building_65_Broadway_entrance.jpg/250px-American_Express_Company_Building_65_Broadway_entrance.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="250" data-height="178" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:American_Express_Company_Building_65_Broadway_entrance.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/65_Broadway" title="65 Broadway">American Express Company Building</a> at 65 Broadway – the former headquarters of the American Express Company</div></div></div>
<p>At the end of the Wells-Fargo reign in 1914, an aggressive new president, George Chadbourne Taylor (1868–1923), who had worked his way up through the company over the previous thirty years, decided to build a new headquarters. The old buildings, dubbed by <a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">the New York Times</a> as "among the ancient landmarks" of lower Broadway, were inadequate for such a rapidly expanding concern.
</p><p>After some delays due to the First World War, the 21-story <a href="/wiki/Neoclassicism" title="Neoclassicism">neo-classical</a> <a href="/wiki/65_Broadway" title="65 Broadway">American Express Co. Building</a> was constructed in 1916–17 to the design of James L. Aspinwall, of the firm of Renwick, Aspinwall &amp; Tucker, the successor to the architectural practice of the eminent <a href="/wiki/James_Renwick,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="James Renwick, Jr.">James Renwick, Jr.</a>. The building consolidated the two lots of the former buildings with a single address: <a href="/wiki/65_Broadway" title="65 Broadway">65 Broadway</a>. This building was part of the "Express Row" section of lower Broadway at the time. The building completed the continuous masonry wall of its block-front and assisted in transforming Broadway into the "canyon" of neo-classical masonry office towers familiar to this day.<sup id="cite_ref-AIA22_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIA22-14">[14]</a></sup> American Express sold this building in 1975, but retained travel services there. The building was also the headquarters over the years of other prominent firms, including investment bankers <a href="/wiki/J._%26_W._Seligman_%26_Co." title="J. &amp; W. Seligman &amp; Co.">J.&amp; W. Seligman &amp; Co</a>. (1940–74), the <a href="/wiki/American_Bureau_of_Shipping" title="American Bureau of Shipping">American Bureau of Shipping</a>, a maritime concern (1977–86), and later J.J. Kenny, and <a href="/wiki/Standard_%26_Poor%27s" title="Standard &amp; Poor's">Standard &amp; Poor's</a>, the latter of which renamed the building for itself.<sup id="cite_ref-LANDMARKS_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LANDMARKS-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AIA22_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AIA22-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Nationwide_expansion">Nationwide expansion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Nationwide expansion" data-section="3" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>American Express extended its reach nationwide by arranging affiliations with other express companies (including <a href="/wiki/History_of_Wells_Fargo" title="History of Wells Fargo">Wells Fargo</a> – the replacement for the two former companies that merged to form American Express), railroads, and steamship companies.<sup id="cite_ref-Citycyclopedia_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Citycyclopedia-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Financial_services">Financial services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Financial services" data-section="4" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In 1857, American Express started its expansion in the area of financial services by launching a <a href="/wiki/Money_order" title="Money order">money order</a> business<sup id="cite_ref-Citycyclopedia_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Citycyclopedia-11">[11]</a></sup> to compete with the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Post_Office" class="mw-redirect" title="United States Post Office">United States Post Office</a>'s money orders.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Amer_express_dog_1890.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Amer_express_dog_1890.jpg/200px-Amer_express_dog_1890.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 200px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Amer_express_dog_1890.jpg/200px-Amer_express_dog_1890.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="200" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Amer_express_dog_1890.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The American Express logo in 1890 depicting a watchdog lying on top of a shipping trunk to symbolise trust and security</div></div></div>
<p>Sometime between 1888 and 1890, <a href="/wiki/J._C._Fargo" title="J. C. Fargo">J. C. Fargo</a> took a trip to Europe and returned frustrated and infuriated. Despite the fact that he was president of American Express and that he carried with him traditional <a href="/wiki/Letter_of_credit" title="Letter of credit">letters of credit</a>, he found it difficult to obtain cash anywhere except in major cities. Fargo went to <a href="/wiki/Marcellus_Flemming_Berry" title="Marcellus Flemming Berry">Marcellus Flemming Berry</a> and asked him to create a better solution than the letter of credit. Berry introduced the American Express <a href="/wiki/Traveler%27s_cheque" title="Traveler's cheque">Traveler's Cheque</a> which was launched in 1891 in denominations of $10, $20, $50, and $100.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p><p>Traveler's cheques established American Express as a truly international company. In 1914, at the onset of <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, American Express in Europe was among the few companies to honor the letters of credit (issued by various banks) held by Americans in Europe, because other financial institutions refused to assist these stranded travelers.
</p><p>The British government appointed American Express its official agent at the beginning of World War I.  They were to deliver letters, money and relief parcels to British prisoners of war.  Their employees went into camps to cash drafts for both British and French prisoners and arranged for them to receive money from home.
</p><p>By the end of the war they were delivering 150 tonnes of parcels per day to prisoners in six countries.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Loss_of_railroad_express_business">Loss of railroad express business</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Loss of railroad express business" data-section="5" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>American Express became one of the monopolies that <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt" title="Theodore Roosevelt">President Theodore Roosevelt</a> had the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a> (ICC) investigated during his administration. The interest of the ICC was drawn to its strict control of the railroad express business. However, the solution did not come immediately to hand.<sup id="cite_ref-Citycyclopedia_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Citycyclopedia-11">[11]</a></sup> The solution to this problem came as a coincidence to other problems during World War I.
</p><p>During the winter of 1917, the United States suffered a severe coal shortage and on December 26 <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Woodrow Wilson</a> commandeered the railroads on behalf of the United States government to move <a href="/wiki/United_States_Armed_Forces" title="United States Armed Forces">federal troops</a>, their supplies, and coal. Treasury Secretary <a href="/wiki/William_Gibbs_McAdoo" title="William Gibbs McAdoo">William Gibbs McAdoo</a> was assigned the task of consolidating the railway lines for the <a href="/wiki/War_effort" title="War effort">war effort</a>. All contracts between express companies and railroads were nullified and McAdoo proposed that all existing express companies be consolidated into a single company to serve the country's needs. This ended American Express's express business, and removed them from the ICC's interest. The result was that a new company called the <a href="/wiki/Railway_Express_Agency" title="Railway Express Agency">American Railway Express Agency</a> formed in July 1918. The new entity took custody of all the pooled equipment and property of existing express companies (the largest share of which, 40%, came from American Express, who had owned the rights to the express business over 71,280 miles (114,710 km) of railroad lines, and had 10,000 offices, with over 30,000 employees).
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Investment_banking">Investment banking</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Investment banking" data-section="6" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>During the 1980s, American Express embarked on an effort to become a financial services supercompany and made a number of acquisitions to create an investment banking arm. In mid-1981 it purchased <a href="/wiki/Sanford_I._Weill" title="Sanford I. Weill">Sanford I. Weill</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Shearson_Loeb_Rhoades" class="mw-redirect" title="Shearson Loeb Rhoades">Shearson Loeb Rhoades</a>, the second largest securities firm in the United States to form <a href="/wiki/Shearson/American_Express" class="mw-redirect" title="Shearson/American Express">Shearson/American Express</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Shearson_Lehman_Logo.png" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/37/Shearson_Lehman_Logo.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="174" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="170" data-file-height="174"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 170px;height: 174px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/37/Shearson_Lehman_Logo.png" data-alt="" data-width="170" data-height="174" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Shearson_Lehman_Logo.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Shearson Lehman logo</div></div></div>
<p>After the purchase of Shearson, Weill was given the position of president of American Express in 1983. Weill grew increasingly unhappy with responsibilities within American Express and his conflicts with American Express' CEO <a href="/wiki/James_D._Robinson_III" title="James D. Robinson III">James D. Robinson III</a>. Weill soon realized that he was not positioned to be named CEO and left in August 1985. In 1984, American Express acquired the <a href="/wiki/Investment_banking" title="Investment banking">investment banking</a> and trading firm <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers Kuhn Loeb</a>, and added it to the Shearson family, creating Shearson Lehman/American Express.  It was Lehman's CEO and former trader <a href="/wiki/Lewis_Glucksman" title="Lewis Glucksman">Lewis Glucksman</a> who would next lead Shearson Lehman/American Express.
</p><p>In 1984, Shearson/American Express purchased the 90-year-old <a href="/wiki/Investors_Diversified_Services" class="mw-redirect" title="Investors Diversified Services">Investors Diversified Services</a>, bringing with it a fleet of financial advisors and investment products. In 1988, Shearson Lehman acquired the brokerage firm <a href="/wiki/E.F._Hutton_%26_Co." class="mw-redirect" title="E.F. Hutton &amp; Co.">E.F. Hutton &amp; Co.</a>. E.F. Hutton was merged with the investment banking business and the investment banking arm was renamed <a href="/wiki/Shearson_Lehman_Hutton,_Inc." class="mw-redirect" title="Shearson Lehman Hutton, Inc.">Shearson Lehman Hutton, Inc.</a><sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p><p>However, when <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Golub" title="Harvey Golub">Harvey Golub</a> became CEO of American Express in 1993, American Express decided to get out of the investment banking business and negotiated the sale of Shearson's retail brokerage and <a href="/wiki/Investment_management" title="Investment management">asset management</a> business to <a href="/wiki/Primerica" title="Primerica">Primerica</a>. The Shearson business was merged with Primerica's <a href="/wiki/Smith_Barney" class="mw-redirect" title="Smith Barney">Smith Barney</a> to create <a href="/wiki/Smith_Barney_Shearson" class="mw-redirect" title="Smith Barney Shearson">Smith Barney Shearson</a>. Ultimately, the Shearson name was dropped in 1994.<sup id="cite_ref-shearsondropped_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shearsondropped-18">[18]</a></sup></p><p>In 1994, American Express spun off of the remaining investment banking and institutional businesses as <a href="/wiki/Lehman_Brothers" title="Lehman Brothers">Lehman Brothers Holdings Inc</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> After almost fifteen years of independence, Lehman Brothers <a href="/wiki/Bankruptcy_of_Lehman_Brothers" title="Bankruptcy of Lehman Brothers">filed for bankruptcy protection in 2008</a> as part of the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%9308" title="Financial crisis of 2007–08">financial crisis of 2007–08</a>.
</p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(2)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history">Recent history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Recent history" data-section="7" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-2 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-2">
<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.<br><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22American+Express%22">"American Express"</a> – <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22American+Express%22+-wikipedia">news</a> <b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22American+Express%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers">newspapers</a> <b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22American+Express%22+-wikipedia">books</a> <b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22American+Express%22">scholar</a> <b>·</b> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22American+Express%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on">JSTOR</a></span></small></span>  <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2019</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table><p>Former CEO <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Chenault" title="Kenneth Chenault">Kenneth Chenault</a> took over leadership of American Express in 2001, from <a href="/wiki/Harvey_Golub" title="Harvey Golub">Harvey Golub</a>, CEO from 1993 to 2001. Prior to that, the company was headed by <a href="/wiki/James_D._Robinson_III" title="James D. Robinson III">James D. Robinson III</a> from 1977 to 1993, <a href="/wiki/Howard_L._Clark_Sr." title="Howard L. Clark Sr.">Howard L. Clark Sr.</a> from 1960 to 1977, and <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Reed_(American_Express)" title="Ralph Reed (American Express)">Ralph Reed</a> from 1944 to 1960. Current CEO: Stephen Joseph Squeri since February 1, 2018.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Charge_card_services">Charge card services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Charge card services" data-section="8" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Financial_Center.jpg" class="image"><noscript><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/World_Financial_Center.jpg/200px-World_Financial_Center.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="242" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="1040" data-file-height="1257"></noscript><span class="lazy-image-placeholder" style="width: 200px;height: 242px;" data-src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/World_Financial_Center.jpg/200px-World_Financial_Center.jpg" data-alt="" data-width="200" data-height="242" data-class="thumbimage"> </span></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:World_Financial_Center.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/American_Express_Tower" class="mw-redirect" title="American Express Tower">American Express Tower</a> (tallest, left) in New York City</div></div></div>
<p>American Express executives discussed the possibility of launching a travel <a href="/wiki/Charge_card" title="Charge card">charge card</a> as early as 1946, but it was not until <a href="/wiki/Diners_Club" class="mw-redirect" title="Diners Club">Diners Club</a> launched a card in March 1950, that American Express began to seriously consider the possibility. At the end of 1957, American Express CEO <a href="/wiki/Ralph_Reed_(American_Express)" title="Ralph Reed (American Express)">Ralph Reed</a> decided to get into the card business, and by the launch date of October 1, 1958, public interest had become so significant that 250,000 cards were issued prior to the official launch date. The card was launched with an annual fee of $6, $1 higher than Diners Club, to be seen as a premium product. The first cards were made of paper, with the account number and cardmember's name typed. In 1959, American Express began issuing embossed <a href="/wiki/ISO/IEC_7810" title="ISO/IEC 7810">ISO/IEC 7810</a> plastic cards, an industry first.<sup id="cite_ref-ourstory_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ourstory-19">[19]</a></sup></p><p>In 1966, American Express introduced the Gold Card.<sup id="cite_ref-ourstory_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ourstory-19">[19]</a></sup> In 1984, the company launched the Platinum Card, clearly defining different <a href="/wiki/Market_segment" class="mw-redirect" title="Market segment">market segments</a> within its own business, a practice that has proliferated across a broad array of industries.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The Platinum Card was billed as super-exclusive and had a $250 annual fee (as of September 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the fee is $550<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>). It was offered by invitation only to American Express customers with at least two years of tenure, significant spending, and excellent payment history; as of 2019<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, it is open to applicants on request. In 1987, American Express introduced the Optima card, its first credit card product that did not have to be paid in full at the end of the month.
</p><p>American Express formed a venture with <a href="/wiki/Warner_Communications" class="mw-redirect" title="Warner Communications">Warner Communications</a> in 1979, called <a href="/wiki/Warner-Amex_Satellite_Entertainment" title="Warner-Amex Satellite Entertainment">Warner-Amex Satellite Entertainment</a>, which created MTV, <a href="/wiki/Nickelodeon" title="Nickelodeon">Nickelodeon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/The_Movie_Channel" title="The Movie Channel">The Movie Channel</a>. The partnership lasted only until 1984. The properties were sold to <a href="/wiki/Viacom_(1971%E2%80%932005)" class="mw-redirect" title="Viacom (1971–2005)">Viacom</a> soon after.
</p><p>In April 1992, American Express spun off its former subsidiary, <a href="/wiki/First_Data_Corp." class="mw-redirect" title="First Data Corp.">First Data Corp.</a>, in an <a href="/wiki/Initial_public_offering" title="Initial public offering">initial public offering</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p><p>In 1994, the Optima True Grace card was introduced. The card was unique in that it offered a <a href="/wiki/Grace_period" title="Grace period">grace period</a> on all purchases whether a balance was carried on the card or not (as opposed to traditional revolving credit cards which charge interest on new purchases if so much as $1 was carried over).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The card was discontinued a few years later; the now discontinued<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (August 2019)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> One from American Express card offered a similar feature called "Interest Protection".
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id=".22Boston_Fee_Party.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='"Boston_Fee_Party"'>"Boston Fee Party"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title='Edit section: "Boston Fee Party"' data-section="9" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>From the early 1980s until the early 1990s, American Express was known for cutting its <a href="/wiki/Interchange_fee" title="Interchange fee">interchange fee</a> (also known as a "discount rate") to merchants and restaurants if they accepted only American Express and no other credit or charge cards. This prompted competitors such as <a href="/wiki/Visa_Inc." title="Visa Inc.">Visa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mastercard" title="Mastercard">Mastercard</a> to cry foul for a while as the tactics "locked" restaurants into American Express. Capitalizing on this elitist image, American Express frequently mentioned such exclusive partnerships in its advertising.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p><p>Aside from some holdouts including <a href="/wiki/Neiman_Marcus" title="Neiman Marcus">Neiman Marcus</a> (which continued exclusivity until 2011), the practice largely ended in 1991. A group of restaurants in <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> stopped accepting American Express while accepting and encouraging the use of Visa and Mastercard, including some that were exclusive to American Express. The rationale was due to far lower fees as compared with American Express' fees at the time (which were about 4% for each transaction versus around 1.2% at the time for Visa and Mastercard). The revolt, known as the "Boston Fee Party" (alluding to the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Tea_Party" title="Boston Tea Party">Boston Tea Party</a>), spread to over 250 restaurants across the United States, including restaurants in other cities such as <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a>. Visa offered to pay the Fee Party's legal bills, and <a href="/wiki/Discover_Card" title="Discover Card">Discover Card</a> was able to increase their acceptance among Boston restaurants by 375%. Kenneth Chenault, then head of Travel Related Services prior to becoming American Express CEO, cut fees to bring these restaurants back into the fold.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> American Express then shifted its focus from exclusivity to broadening acceptance, adding mainstream merchants like <a href="/wiki/Walmart" title="Walmart">Walmart</a> to the American Express network.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Conversion_to_bank_holding_company">Conversion to bank holding company</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Conversion to bank holding company" data-section="10" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>On November 10, 2008, during the <a href="/wiki/Financial_crisis_of_2007%E2%80%932008" class="mw-redirect" title="Financial crisis of 2007–2008">financial crisis of 2007–2008</a>, the company won <a href="/wiki/Federal_Reserve_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Reserve System">Federal Reserve System</a> approval to convert to a <a href="/wiki/Bank_holding_company" title="Bank holding company">bank holding company</a>, making it eligible for government help under the <a href="/wiki/Troubled_Asset_Relief_Program" title="Troubled Asset Relief Program">Troubled Asset Relief Program</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-American-Express-Dec-2008-8-K_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American-Express-Dec-2008-8-K-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-lanman_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanman-27">[27]</a></sup> At that time, American Express had total consolidated assets of about $127<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion.<sup id="cite_ref-lanman_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lanman-27">[27]</a></sup> In June 2009, $3.39<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion in TARP funds were repaid plus $74.4<span class="nowrap"> </span>million in dividend payments. In July 2009, the company ended its obligations under TARP by buying back $340<span class="nowrap"> </span>million in Treasury warrants.<sup id="cite_ref-American-Express-Jul-2009-8-K_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-American-Express-Jul-2009-8-K-28">[28]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Charging_order_controversy_in_the_UK">Charging order controversy in the UK</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Charging order controversy in the UK" data-section="11" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In November 2010, the UK division of American Express was cautioned by the <a href="/wiki/Office_of_Fair_Trading" title="Office of Fair Trading">Office of Fair Trading</a> for the use of controversial <a href="/wiki/Charging_order" title="Charging order">charging orders</a> against those in debt.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The regulator said that the company was one of four companies who were encouraging customers to turn their unsecured credit card debts into a form of secured debt.
</p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="CFPB_enforcement_action">CFPB enforcement action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: CFPB enforcement action" data-section="12" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>In October 2012, The <a href="/wiki/Consumer_Financial_Protection_Bureau" title="Consumer Financial Protection Bureau">Consumer Financial Protection Bureau</a> (CFPB) announced an enforcement action with orders requiring three American Express subsidiaries to refund an estimated $85<span class="nowrap"> </span>million to approximately 250,000 customers for illegal card practices between 2003 and 2012. Allegations included that American Express made misleading statements regarding signup bonuses, charged unlawful late fees, discriminated against applicants due to age, and failed to report consumer complaints to regulators.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span id="Costco_exclusivity_arrangement_.282004.E2.80.932016.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Costco_exclusivity_arrangement_(2004–2016)">Costco exclusivity arrangement (2004–2016)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Costco exclusivity arrangement (2004–2016)" data-section="13" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>Beginning in 2004, <a href="/wiki/Costco" title="Costco">Costco</a> issued co-branded American Express cards which also doubled as a membership card. Costco ended this arrangement in 2016, in the United States and in 2015, in Canada. The cards did not have annual fees and offered cash back in form of in-store rebates on certain tiers of purchases. The TrueEarnings cards issued by Costco in the United States were an extension of an exclusive credit card network deal between Costco and American Express dating from 1999. Costco was the last major U.S. merchant that accepted American Express cards exclusively. In November 2011, <a href="/wiki/Neiman_Marcus" title="Neiman Marcus">Neiman Marcus</a>, which gave similar general-purpose card exclusivity to American Express since 1987, began accepting Visa and <a href="/wiki/Mastercard" title="Mastercard">Mastercard</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (September 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p><p>Costco's Canadian stores ended its exclusive deal with American Express in January 2015, in favor of one with <a href="/wiki/Capital_One" title="Capital One">Capital One</a> and Mastercard.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Capital One did not buy accounts and balances from American Express, which required Costco Canada members to apply for the new cards instead of automatically qualifying.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p><p>On February 12, 2015, it was announced that the partnership between American Express and Costco in the United States would dissolve March 31, 2016, which was later extended to June 19, 2016. By March 2, 2015, Costco announced that <a href="/wiki/Citigroup" title="Citigroup">Citigroup</a> would become the exclusive issuer of Costco's credit cards and that <a href="/wiki/Visa_Inc." title="Visa Inc.">Visa Inc.</a> would replace American Express as the exclusive credit card network accepted at Costco's stores in the United States. The Costco deal with Visa began on June 20, 2016, and in addition to the new Citi card, Costco accepted all other Visa cards.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Los_Angeles_Times_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Los_Angeles_Times-36">[36]</a></sup> All TrueEarnings card accounts and balances held by American Express were sold to Citigroup, and new Costco Anywhere Visa cards were sent to Costco members prior to the switch date. Concurrent with the switch to Visa, Costco no longer accepted American Express in stores, at Costco.com, or through Costco Travel.
</p><p>The Costco partnership represented 8%, or $80<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion, of American Express' billed business and about 20%, or about $14<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion, of its interest-bearing credit portfolio, according to Richard Shane of JPMorgan Chase &amp; Co.<sup id="cite_ref-Los_Angeles_Times_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Los_Angeles_Times-36">[36]</a></sup> The impact of losing the Costco card accounts was significant; in the first quarter without Costco cards, company profit dropped 10% and revenue dropped 5% compared to the previous year.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3 class="in-block"><span class="mw-headline" id="Centurion_Lounges">Centurion Lounges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Centurion Lounges" data-section="14" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h3>
<p>American Express has a network of Centurion Lounges at major airports to cater to their Platinum and Centurion card holders.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Free services include concierge, food and alcohol, wifi, etc. The first lounge opened in 2013, at <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas_McCarran_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Las Vegas McCarran Airport">McCarran Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> In 2019 American Express acquired lounge app LoungeBuddy <sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
</section><h2 class="section-heading" onclick="javascript:mfTempOpenSection(3)"><div class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element indicator mw-ui-icon-small mw-ui-icon-flush-left"></div><span class="mw-headline" id="Finances">Finances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Express&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Finances" data-section="15" class="mw-ui-icon mw-ui-icon-element mw-ui-icon-wikimedia-edit-base20 edit-page mw-ui-icon-flush-right">Edit</a></span></h2><section class="mf-section-3 collapsible-block" id="mf-section-3">
<p>For the fiscal year 2017, American Express reported earnings of US$2.634<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion, with an annual revenue of US$35.583<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion, an increase of 5.2% over the previous fiscal cycle. Its shares traded at over $83 per share, and its market capitalization was valued at over US$86.5<span class="nowrap"> </span>billion in October 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> American Express ranked <abbr title="Number">#</abbr>86 in the 2018 <a href="/wiki/Fortune_500" title="Fortune 500">Fortune 500</a> list of the largest United States corporations by total revenue.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
